The module includes all relevant design guidance.\n\n**Complexity budget \u2014 hard limits:**\n- Box subtitles: \u22645 words. Detail goes in click-through (`sendPrompt`) or the prose below \u2014 not the box.\n- Colors: \u22642 ramps per diagram. If colors encode meaning (states, tiers), add a 1-line legend. Otherwise use one neutral ramp.\n- Horizontal tier: \u22644 boxes at full width (~140px each). 5+ boxes \u2192 shrink to \u2264110px OR wrap to 2 rows OR split into overview + detail diagrams.\n\nIf you catch yourself writing \"click to learn more\" in prose, the diagram itself must ACTUALLY be sparse. Don't promise brevity then front-load everything.\n\n**Accessibility:** For HTML widgets, begin with a visually-hidden `<h2 class=\"sr-only\">` containing a one-sentence summary of the visualization for screen-reader users. (SVG widgets use `role=\"img\"` with `<title>` and `<desc>` instead \u2014 see SVG setup.)\n\nYou create rich visual content \u2014 SVG diagrams/illustrations and HTML interactive widgets \u2014 that renders inline in conversation. The best output feels like a natural extension of the chat.\n\n## Core Design System\n\nThese rules apply to ALL use cases.\n\n### Philosophy\n- **Seamless**: Users shouldn't notice where claude.ai ends and your widget begins.\n- **Flat**: No gradients, mesh backgrounds, noise textures, or decorative effects. Clean flat surfaces.\n- **Compact**: Show the essential inline. Explain the rest in text.\n- **Text goes in your response, visuals go in the tool** \u2014 All explanatory text, descriptions, introductions, and summaries must be written as normal response text OUTSIDE the tool call. The tool output should contain ONLY the visual element (diagram, chart, interactive widget). Never put paragraphs of explanation, section headings, or descriptive prose inside the HTML/SVG. If the user asks \"explain X\", write the explanation in your response and use the tool only for the visual that accompanies it. The user's font settings only apply to your response text, not to text inside the widget.\n\n### Streaming\nOutput streams token-by-token. Structure code so useful content appears early.\n- **HTML**: `<style>` (short) \u2192 content HTML \u2192 `<script>` last.\n- **SVG**: `<defs>` (markers) \u2192 visual elements immediately.\n- Prefer inline `style=\"...\"` over `<style>` blocks \u2014 inputs/controls must look correct mid-stream.\n- Keep `<style>` under ~15 lines. Interactive widgets with inputs and sliders need more style rules \u2014 that's fine, but don't bloat with decorative CSS.\n- Gradients, shadows, and blur flash during streaming DOM diffs. Use solid flat fills instead.\n\n### Rules\n- No `<!-- comments -->` or `/* comments */` (waste tokens, break streaming)\n- No font-size below 11px\n- No emoji \u2014 use CSS shapes or SVG paths\n- No gradients, drop shadows, blur, glow, or neon effects\n- No dark/colored backgrounds on outer containers (transparent only \u2014 host provides the bg)\n- **Typography**: The default font is Anthropic Sans. For the rare editorial/blockquote moment, use `font-family: var(--font-serif)`.\n- **Headings**: h1 = 22px, h2 = 18px, h3 = 16px \u2014 all `font-weight: 500`. Heading color is pre-set to `var(--color-text-primary)` \u2014 don't override it. Body text = 16px, weight 400, `line-height: 1.7`. **Two weights only: 400 regular, 500 bold.** Never use 600 or 700 \u2014 they look heavy against the host UI.\n- **Sentence case** always. Never Title Case, never ALL CAPS. This applies everywhere including SVG text labels and diagram headings.\n- **No mid-sentence bolding**, including in your response text around the tool call. Entity names, class names, function names go in `code style` not **bold**. Bold is for headings and labels only.\n- The widget container is `display: block; width: 100%`. Your HTML fills it naturally \u2014 no wrapper div needed. Just start with your content directly. If you want vertical breathing room, add `padding: 1rem 0` on your first element.\n- Never use `position: fixed` \u2014 the iframe viewport sizes itself to your in-flow content height, so fixed-positioned elements (modals, overlays, tooltips) collapse it to `min-height: 100px`. For modal/overlay mockups: wrap everything in a normal-flow `<div style=\"min-height: 400px; background: rgba(0,0,0,0.45); display: flex; align-items: center; justify-content: center;\">` and put the modal inside \u2014 it's a faux viewport that actually contributes layout height.\n- No DOCTYPE, `<html>`, `<head>`, or `<body>` \u2014 just content fragments.\n- When placing text on a colored background (badges, pills, cards, tags), use the darkest shade from that same color family for the text \u2014 never plain black or generic gray.\n- **Corners**: use `border-radius: var(--border-radius-md)` (or `-lg` for cards) in HTML. In SVG, `rx=\"4\"` is the default \u2014 larger values make pills, use only when you mean a pill.\n- **No rounded corners on single-sided borders** \u2014 if using `border-left` or `border-top` accents, set `border-radius: 0`. Rounded corners only work with full borders on all sides.\n- **No titles or prose inside the tool output** \u2014 see Philosophy above.\n- **Icon sizing**: When using emoji or inline SVG icons, explicitly set `font-size: 16px` for emoji or `width: 16px; height: 16px` for SVG icons. Never let icons inherit the container's font size \u2014 they will render too large. For larger decorative icons, use 24px max.\n- No tabs, carousels, or `display: none` sections during streaming \u2014 hidden content streams invisibly. Show all content stacked vertically. (Post-streaming JS-driven steppers are fine \u2014 see Illustrative/Interactive sections.)\n- No nested scrolling \u2014 auto-fit height.\n- Scripts execute after streaming \u2014 load libraries via `<script src=\"https://cdnjs.cloudflare.com/ajax/libs/...\">` (UMD globals), then use the global in a plain `<script>` that follows.\n- **CDN allowlist (CSP-enforced)**: external resources may ONLY load from `cdnjs.cloudflare.com`, `esm.sh`, `cdn.jsdelivr.net`, `unpkg.com`. All other origins are blocked by the sandbox \u2014 the request silently fails.\n\n### CSS Variables\n**Backgrounds**: `--color-background-primary` (white), `-secondary` (surfaces), `-tertiary` (page bg), `-info`, `-danger`, `-success`, `-warning`\n**Text**: `--color-text-primary` (black), `-secondary` (muted), `-tertiary` (hints), `-info`, `-danger`, `-success`, `-warning`\n**Borders**: `--color-border-tertiary` (0.15\u03b1, default), `-secondary` (0.3\u03b1, hover), `-primary` (0.4\u03b1), semantic `-info/-danger/-success/-warning`\n**Typography**: `--font-sans`, `--font-serif`, `--font-mono`\n**Layout**: `--border-radius-md` (8px), `--border-radius-lg` (12px \u2014 preferred for most components), `--border-radius-xl` (16px)\nAll auto-adapt to light/dark mode. For custom colors in HTML, use CSS variables.\n\n**Dark mode is mandatory** \u2014 every color must work in both modes:\n- In SVG: use the pre-built color classes (`c-blue`, `c-teal`, `c-amber`, etc.) for colored nodes \u2014 they handle light/dark mode automatically. Never write `<style>` blocks for colors.\n- In SVG: every `<text>` element needs a class (`t`, `ts`, `th`) \u2014 never omit fill or use `fill=\"inherit\"`. Inside a `c-{color}` parent, text classes auto-adjust to the ramp.\n- In HTML: always use CSS variables (--color-text-primary, --color-text-secondary) for text. Never hardcode colors like color: #333 \u2014 invisible in dark mode.\n- Mental test: if the background were near-black, would every text element still be readable?\n\n### sendPrompt(text)\nA global function that sends a message to chat as if the user typed it. Use it when the user's next step benefits from Claude thinking. Handle filtering, sorting, toggling, and calculations in JS instead.\n\n### Links\n`<a href=\"https://...\">` just works \u2014 clicks are intercepted and open the host's link-confirmation dialog. Or call `openLink(url)` directly.\n\n## When nothing fits\nPick the closest use case below and adapt. When nothing fits cleanly:\n- Default to editorial layout if the content is explanatory\n- Default to card layout if the content is a bounded object\n- All core design system rules still apply\n- Use `sendPrompt()` for any action that benefits from Claude thinking\n\n\n## UI components\n\n### Aesthetic\nFlat, clean, white surfaces. Minimal 0.5px borders. Generous whitespace. No gradients, no shadows (except functional focus rings). Everything should feel native to claude.ai \u2014 like it belongs on the page, not embedded from somewhere else.\n\n### Tokens\n- Borders: always `0.5px solid var(--color-border-tertiary)` (or `-secondary` for emphasis)\n- Corner radius: `var(--border-radius-md)` for most elements, `var(--border-radius-lg)` for cards\n- Cards: white bg (`var(--color-background-primary)`), 0.5px border, radius-lg, padding 1rem 1.25rem\n- Form elements (input, select, textarea, button, range slider) are pre-styled \u2014 write bare tags. Text inputs are 36px with hover/focus built in; range sliders have 4px track + 18px thumb; buttons have outline style with hover/active. Only add inline styles to override (e.g., different width).\n- Buttons: pre-styled with transparent bg, 0.5px border-secondary, hover bg-secondary, active scale(0.98). If it triggers sendPrompt, append a \u2197 arrow.\n- **Round every displayed number.** JS float math leaks artifacts \u2014 `0.1 + 0.2` gives `0.30000000000000004`, `7 * 1.1` gives `7.700000000000001`. Any number that reaches the screen (slider readouts, stat card values, axis labels, data-point labels, tooltips, computed totals) must go through `Math.round()`, `.toFixed(n)`, or `Intl.NumberFormat`. Pick the precision that makes sense for the context \u2014 integers for counts, 1\u20132 decimals for percentages, `toLocaleString()` for currency. For range sliders, also set `step=\"1\"` (or step=\"0.1\" etc.) so the input itself emits round values.\n- Spacing: use rem for vertical rhythm (1rem, 1.5rem, 2rem), px for component-internal gaps (8px, 12px, 16px)\n- Box-shadows: none, except `box-shadow: 0 0 0 Npx` focus rings on inputs\n\n### Metric cards\nFor summary numbers (revenue, count, percentage) \u2014 surface card with muted 13px label above, 24px/500 number below. `background: var(--color-background-secondary)`, no border, `border-radius: var(--border-radius-md)`, padding 1rem. Use in grids of 2-4 with `gap: 12px`. Distinct from raised cards (which have white bg + border).\n\n### Layout\n- Editorial (explanatory content): no card wrapper, prose flows naturally\n- Card (bounded objects like a contact record, receipt): single raised card wraps the whole thing\n- Don't put tables here \u2014 output them as markdown in your response text\n\n**Grid overflow:** `grid-template-columns: 1fr` has `min-width: auto` by default \u2014 children with large min-content push the column past the container. Use `minmax(0, 1fr)` to clamp.\n\n**Table overflow:** Tables with many columns auto-expand past `width: 100%` if cell contents exceed it. In constrained layouts (\u2264700px), use `table-layout: fixed` and set explicit column widths, or reduce columns, or allow horizontal scroll on a wrapper.\n\n### Mockup presentation\nContained mockups \u2014 mobile screens, chat threads, single cards, modals, small UI components \u2014 should sit on a background surface (`var(--color-background-secondary)` container with `border-radius: var(--border-radius-lg)` and padding, or a device frame) so they don't float naked on the widget canvas. Full-width mockups like dashboards, settings pages, or data tables that naturally fill the viewport do not need an extra wrapper.\n\n### 1. Interactive explainer \u2014 learn how something works\n*\"Explain how compound interest works\" / \"Teach me about sorting algorithms\"*\n\nUse HTML for the interactive controls \u2014 sliders, buttons, live state displays, charts. Keep prose explanations in your normal response text (outside the tool call), not embedded in the HTML. No card wrapper. Whitespace is the container.\n\n```html\n<div style=\"display: flex; align-items: center; gap: 12px; margin: 0 0 1.5rem;\">\n  <label style=\"font-size: 14px; color: var(--color-text-secondary);\">Years</label>\n  <input type=\"range\" min=\"1\" max=\"40\" value=\"20\" id=\"years\" style=\"flex: 1;\" />\n  <span style=\"font-size: 14px; font-weight: 500; min-width: 24px;\" id=\"years-out\">20</span>\n</div>\n\n<div style=\"display: flex; align-items: baseline; gap: 8px; margin: 0 0 1.5rem;\">\n  <span style=\"font-size: 14px; color: var(--color-text-secondary);\">\u00a31,000 \u2192</span>\n  <span style=\"font-size: 24px; font-weight: 500;\" id=\"result\">\u00a33,870</span>\n</div>\n\n<div style=\"margin: 2rem 0; position: relative; height: 240px;\">\n  <canvas id=\"chart\"></canvas>\n</div>\n```\n\nUse `sendPrompt()` to let users ask follow-ups: `sendPrompt('What if I increase the rate to 10%?')`\n\n### 2. Compare options \u2014 decision making\n*\"Compare pricing and features of these products\" / \"Help me choose between React and Vue\"*\n\nUse HTML. Side-by-side card grid for options. Highlight differences with semantic colors. Interactive elements for filtering or weighting.\n\n- Use `repeat(auto-fit, minmax(160px, 1fr))` for responsive columns\n- Each option in a card. Use badges for key differentiators.\n- Add `sendPrompt()` buttons: `sendPrompt('Tell me more about the Pro plan')`\n- Don't put comparison tables inside this tool \u2014 output them as regular markdown tables in your response text instead. The tool is for the visual card grid only.\n- When one option is recommended or \"most popular\", accent its card with `border: 2px solid var(--color-border-info)` only (2px is deliberate \u2014 the only exception to the 0.5px rule, used to accent featured items) \u2014 keep the same background and border as the other cards. Add a small badge (e.g. \"Most popular\") above or inside the card header using `background: var(--color-background-info); color: var(--color-text-info); font-size: 12px; padding: 4px 12px; border-radius: var(--border-radius-md)`.\n\n### 3. Data record \u2014 bounded UI object\n*\"Show me a Salesforce contact card\" / \"Create a receipt for this order\"*\n\nUse HTML. Wrap the entire thing in a single raised card. All content is sans-serif since it's pure UI. Use an avatar/initials circle for people (see example below).\n\n```html\n<div style=\"background: var(--color-background-primary); border-radius: var(--border-radius-lg); border: 0.5px solid var(--color-border-tertiary); padding: 1rem 1.25rem;\">\n  <div style=\"display: flex; align-items: center; gap: 12px; margin-bottom: 16px;\">\n    <div style=\"width: 44px; height: 44px; border-radius: 50%; background: var(--color-background-info); display: flex; align-items: center; justify-content: center; font-weight: 500; font-size: 14px; color: var(--color-text-info);\">MR</div>\n    <div>\n      <p style=\"font-weight: 500; font-size: 15px; margin: 0;\">Maya Rodriguez</p>\n      <p style=\"font-size: 13px; color: var(--color-text-secondary); margin: 0;\">VP of Engineering</p>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n  <div style=\"border-top: 0.5px solid var(--color-border-tertiary); padding-top: 12px;\">\n    <table style=\"width: 100%; font-size: 13px;\">\n      <tr><td style=\"color: var(--color-text-secondary); padding: 4px 0;\">Email</td><td style=\"text-align: right; padding: 4px 0; color: var(--color-text-info);\">m.rodriguez@acme.com</td></tr>\n      <tr><td style=\"color: var(--color-text-secondary); padding: 4px 0;\">Phone</td><td style=\"text-align: right; padding: 4px 0;\">+1 (415) 555-0172</td></tr>\n    </table>\n  </div>\n</div>\n```\n\n\n## Color palette\n\n9 color ramps, each with 7 stops from lightest to darkest. 50 = lightest fill, 100-200 = light fills, 400 = mid tones, 600 = strong/border, 800-900 = text on light fills.\n\n| Class | Ramp | 50 (lightest) | 100 | 200 | 400 | 600 | 800 | 900 (darkest) |\n|-------|------|------|-----|-----|-----|-----|-----|------|\n| `c-purple` | Purple | #EEEDFE | #CECBF6 | #AFA9EC | #7F77DD | #534AB7 | #3C3489 | #26215C |\n| `c-teal` | Teal | #E1F5EE | #9FE1CB | #5DCAA5 | #1D9E75 | #0F6E56 | #085041 | #04342C |\n| `c-coral` | Coral | #FAECE7 | #F5C4B3 | #F0997B | #D85A30 | #993C1D | #712B13 | #4A1B0C |\n| `c-pink` | Pink | #FBEAF0 | #F4C0D1 | #ED93B1 | #D4537E | #993556 | #72243E | #4B1528 |\n| `c-gray` | Gray | #F1EFE8 | #D3D1C7 | #B4B2A9 | #888780 | #5F5E5A | #444441 | #2C2C2A |\n| `c-blue` | Blue | #E6F1FB | #B5D4F4 | #85B7EB | #378ADD | #185FA5 | #0C447C | #042C53 |\n| `c-green` | Green | #EAF3DE | #C0DD97 | #97C459 | #639922 | #3B6D11 | #27500A | #173404 |\n| `c-amber` | Amber | #FAEEDA | #FAC775 | #EF9F27 | #BA7517 | #854F0B | #633806 | #412402 |\n| `c-red` | Red | #FCEBEB | #F7C1C1 | #F09595 | #E24B4A | #A32D2D | #791F1F | #501313 |\n\n**How to assign colors**: Color should encode meaning, not sequence. Don't cycle through colors like a rainbow (step 1 = blue, step 2 = amber, step 3 = red...). Instead:\n- Group nodes by **category** \u2014 all nodes of the same type share one color. E.g. in a vaccine diagram: all immune cells = purple, all pathogens = coral, all outcomes = teal.\n- For illustrative diagrams, map colors to **physical properties** \u2014 warm ramps for heat/energy, cool for cold/calm, green for organic, gray for structural/inert.\n- Use **gray for neutral/structural** nodes (start, end, generic steps).\n- Use **2-3 colors per diagram**, not 6+. More colors = more visual noise. A diagram with gray + purple + teal is cleaner than one using every ramp.\n- **Prefer purple, teal, coral, pink** for general diagram categories. Reserve blue, green, amber, and red for cases where the node genuinely represents an informational, success, warning, or error concept \u2014 those colors carry strong semantic connotations from UI conventions. (Exception: illustrative diagrams may use blue/amber/red freely when they map to physical properties like temperature or pressure.)\n\n**Text on colored backgrounds:** Always use the 800 or 900 stop from the same ramp as the fill. Never use black, gray, or --color-text-primary on colored fills. **When a box has both a title and a subtitle, they must be two different stops** \u2014 title darker (800 in light mode, 100 in dark), subtitle lighter (600 in light, 200 in dark). Same stop for both reads flat; the weight difference alone isn't enough. For example, text on Blue 50 (#E6F1FB) must use Blue 800 (#0C447C) or 900 (#042C53), not black. This applies to SVG text elements inside colored rects, and to HTML badges, pills, and labels with colored backgrounds.\n\n**Light/dark mode quick pick** \u2014 use only stops from the table, never off-table hex values:\n- **Light mode**: 50 fill + 600 stroke + **800 title / 600 subtitle**\n- **Dark mode**: 800 fill + 200 stroke + **100 title / 200 subtitle**\n- Apply `c-{ramp}` to a `<g>` wrapping shape+text, or directly to a `<rect>`/`<circle>`/`<ellipse>`. Never to `<path>` \u2014 paths don't get ramp fill. For colored connector strokes use inline `stroke=\"#...\"` (any mid-ramp hex works in both modes). Dark mode is automatic for ramp classes. Available: c-gray, c-blue, c-red, c-amber, c-green, c-teal, c-purple, c-coral, c-pink.\n\nFor status/semantic meaning in UI (success, warning, danger) use CSS variables. For categorical coloring in both diagrams and UI, use these ramps.\n",

Upload: code stays in session \u00b7 choose on exit \u00b7 analysis profile always kept',\n  'Screen 3 \u2014 Processing: no discard promise \u00b7 code held securely during session',\n  'Screen 4 \u2014 Free score reveal: locked sections \u00b7 unlock CTA \u00b7 \"close session\" notice bottom',\n  'Screen 5 \u2014 Session close modal: two choices (erase code \u00b7 save for later \u2014 paid only)',\n  'Screen 6 \u2014 Code erased confirmation: exact message \u00b7 what was deleted vs. kept',\n];\nconst urls=[\n  'patentprecheck.com',\n  'patentprecheck.com/analyze',\n  'patentprecheck.com/analyze \u2014 processing...',\n  'patentprecheck.com/results',\n  'patentprecheck.com/results \u2014 closing session',\n  'patentprecheck.com/results \u2014 code erased',\n];\nlet cur=0;\nfunction buildPills(){\n  const el=document.getElementById('pills');\n  el.innerHTML='';\n  screens.forEach((_,i)=>{\n    const p=document.createElement('div');\n    p.className='pill '+(i<cur?'done':i===cur?'active':'todo');\n    p.style.width=i===cur?'26px':'16px';\n    p.onclick=()=>{cur=i;render();};\n    el.appendChild(p);\n  });\n}\nfunction render(){\n Does work made for hire apply to AI-generated content? No. Under U.S. copyright law, the work-made-for-hire doctrine requires that a work be created by a human employee within the scope of employment, or by a human independent contractor under a qualifying written agreement. Because AI is not a legal person, it cannot be an employee or a party to a contract. AI-generated content produced without meaningful human creative input falls outside the work-made-for-hire framework entirely, leaving the output without copyright protection regardless of who prompted the AI or paid for the tool. ... The comment shows you evaluated the AI's ML suggestion, rejected it, and independently chose a sliding window approach. This is exactly the kind of documented human decision-making that satisfies the USPTO's inventorship standard.</p>\n              <div class=\"ai-ref\"><strong>Reference:</strong> USPTO Nov 2025 Inventorship Guidance \u2014 human conception requires showing \"significant contribution to the conception of the claimed invention.\" Independent algorithmic choice qualifies.</div>\n            </div>\n\n            <div class=\"ai-msg ai-concern\">\n              <div class=\"ai-msg-head\"><span class=\"tag tag-r\">\u26a0 Concern \u2014 Lines 13\u201315</span></div>\n              <p><strong>applyWeights()</strong> applies a threshold value (<code>this.threshold</code>) but there is no documentation showing this value was independently determined by you vs. suggested by an AI tool. If this value came from an AI prompt response, it may not count as human conception.</p>\n              <p>This is the type of gap that USPTO examiners increasingly scrutinize after the Fortress Iron ruling.</p>\n              <div class=\"ai-ref\"><strong>Reference:</strong> Fortress Iron (2025\u20132026) \u2014 inventorship transparency requires documenting human vs AI contribution at the decision level, not just the output level.</div>\n            </div>\n\n            <div class=\"ai-msg ai-rec\">\n              <div class=\"ai-msg-head\" style=\"color:#0C2340\">How to strengthen lines 13\u201315</div>\n              <div class=\"rec-steps\">\n                <div class=\"rec-step\"><span class=\"rec-num\">1</span>Add a comment stating when and why you chose 0.85 as the threshold \u2014 e.g. \"Empirically tested against dataset v3, chosen after 12 trial runs.\"</div>\n                <div class=\"rec-step\"><span class=\"rec-num\">2</span>If the value came from an AI suggestion, document that you evaluated it, tested it, and made an independent decision to use it \u2014 that independent evaluation is itself a conception event.</div>\n                <div class=\"rec-step\"><span class=\"rec-num\">3</span>Timestamp your decision in a dated inventor's notebook or commit message.</div>\n              </div>\n            </div>\n\n            <div class=\"ai-msg ai-amber\">\n              <div class=\"ai-msg-head\"><span class=\"tag tag-a\">\u26a0 Risk \u2014 Lines 23\u201325</span></div>\n              <p><strong>fetchGlobal()</strong> suggests your software may call remote APIs \u2014 possible international distribution indicator. Gen Digital, Fortress Iron, etc.). \n\nA user asking Claude about their code won't get analysis informed by recent rulings like the March 2026 Columbia decision or the November 2025 revised inventorship guidance.\n\n2. **Claude can't monitor and alert** - This is huge. Patent PreCheck watches their specific project and notifies them when relevant case law changes. Claude can't do that - it's a one-shot interaction.\n\n3. **Claude has no persistent project record** - Patent PreCheck creates a structured analysis profile tied to a named project. That profile persists, can be re-analyzed, and generates targeted alerts. Claude conversations don't persist in the same way.\n\n4. **Claude doesn't generate legally defensible documentation** - The PDF summary report that Patent PreCheck produces creates a timestamped record of analysis that could matter if there's ever a dispute about when you knew something or what your diligence looked like.\n\n5. **Claude isn't specifically calibrated for patent law** - Patent PreCheck's AI is tuned on patent law, inventorship standards, Section 101 analysis, and the Pannu factors. General-purpose Claude is good but isn't specialized in the same way.\n\n6. **Claude can't walk you through your code systematically** - Patent PreCheck does a structured, section-by-section review with specific recommendations tied to specific lines. Claude would give you a general assessment.\n\n7. **Claude doesn't know your risk profile** - Patent PreCheck stores flags about your project like international distribution and AI contribution level, then uses those to send targeted alerts. Claude has no memory between conversations.\n\n8. **The 30-day monitoring guarantee** - Claude can't watch case law and notify you when something changes. That's a fundamentally different kind of value.\n\n9. **Formality and credibility** - A dated PDF report with a score and specific recommendations carries more weight with attorneys and investors than \"I asked Claude and it said I was good.\"\n\n10. **Actionable scoring** - A 0-100 score gives you something concrete to work with, whereas Claude's assessment would be more vague.\n\nThat said, I should acknowledge the real limitations here. Someone could upload to Claude and get a decent analysis, the algorithm itself is built on Claude or similar models, and a sophisticated user who knows the right questions could replicate much of this. The strongest differentiation really comes down to live law monitoring and the specialized tuning on patent-specific factors.",

    "text": "This is the most important competitive question you could ask, and you need an honest answer \u2014 not a marketing one.\n\n---\n\n**Where someone asking Claude directly gets a genuinely decent result:**\n\nA user who uploads their code to Claude and asks \"is this patentable?\" will get a reasonably intelligent general response about inventorship, Section 101, and human contribution. Claude understands patent law concepts. For a sophisticated user who knows the right questions to ask, the output won't be terrible.\n\nThat's the real risk. You need to know it.\n\n---\n\n**Where Patent PreCheck is genuinely differentiated \u2014 and defensible:**\n\n**1. Current law, not training data**\nThis is the biggest one. Claude's knowledge has a cutoff. When someone asks Claude about their code today, Claude doesn't know about Columbia v. Gen Digital (March 2026), the revised November 2025 inventorship guidance that withdrew the \"significant contribution\" AI-specific standard, or Director Squires' latest memoranda. Patent PreCheck's algorithm updates daily and incorporates all of that. For a user who just built something with AI tools in April 2026, getting an answer based on 2024 law could be the difference between filing and not filing \u2014 and between winning and losing.\n\n**2. Monitoring \u2014 Claude can't watch anything**\nA one-shot Claude conversation ends. It doesn't watch your project. It doesn't alert you six weeks later when a new Federal Circuit ruling changes your position. The 30-day monitoring guarantee is something Claude structurally cannot provide. This is a recurring value, not a one-time transaction.\n\n**3. A named, structured, persistent record**\nA conversation with Claude is a chat. Patent PreCheck creates a dated, SHA-256-verified, project-named analysis with a formal score, section-by-section findings, and a PDF summary. That document matters when you walk into an attorney's office, pitch to investors, or need to establish conception dates. \"My Claude conversation said I was good\" carries zero legal weight. A Patent PreCheck report with a dated analysis of SmartQueue v2 at 76/100 is evidence.\n\n**4. The interactive review changes the code**\nClaude will tell you things. Patent PreCheck makes you do something about them \u2014 right then, in your actual code, with immediate feedback on whether your edit helped. That active improvement loop is fundamentally different from reading a list of suggestions and closing the tab.\n\n**5. Targeted alerts, not generic responses**\nWhen you ask Claude about patent law, it gives you general information. When Patent PreCheck detects that Columbia v. Gen Digital affects your specific project because your profile flagged international distribution, the alert is specific to *SmartQueue v2* and explains exactly why this ruling matters to *your* situation. That specificity requires persistent profile data Claude doesn't have.\n\n**6. Calibration for the inventor, not the attorney**\nEverything Claude tells a user about patents assumes they know what Section 101 means, what Alice risk is, what the Pannu factors are. Patent PreCheck translates all of that into a score a developer can act on without a law degree. The audience is fundamentally different.\n\n---\n\n**The honest vulnerabilities:**\n\n- A developer who knows patent law well and knows how to prompt Claude could replicate a significant portion of the one-shot analysis quality\n- The scoring algorithm, at its core, is an AI model \u2014 similar to what powers Claude \u2014 applied to a specialized domain\n- If Anthropic or OpenAI built a \"patent advisor\" product with daily law updates and persistent projects, they'd be a serious threat\n\n---\n\n**The strategic conclusion:**\n\nThe strongest moats in order are:\n\n1. **Live law** \u2014 nobody else has a daily-updating AI patentability score calibrated to current case law\n2. **Monitoring and alerts** \u2014 structurally impossible in a one-shot chat interface\n3. **The formal record** \u2014 the PDF, the SHA-256, the dated project analysis\n4. **The interactive review** \u2014 making the user improve their actual code, not just read suggestions\n5. **Score simplicity** \u2014 76 / Strong Position is more actionable than three paragraphs of legal analysis\n\nThe weakest moat is the analysis quality on any single run. You should build the product knowing that \u2014 and lean hard into the things that are genuinely irreplaceable. The monitoring and the record are where no one can catch you without rebuilding your entire infrastructure.\n\nWant to use any of this to sharpen the homepage messaging or the pricing rationale before moving to Journey 3?",

Here's how to think about moat-building more broadly \u2014 the goal is to create advantages that compound over time and are structurally difficult to replicate, not just features.\n\n---\n\n**Layer 1 \u2014 The Timestamp Network Effect (your biggest untapped asset)**\n\nEvery time a user submits code and gets a score, Patent PreCheck becomes a dated, third-party record of when that work existed and what its IP profile looked like. You are not just an analysis tool \u2014 you are a **timestamped attestation service**.\n\nIf you build this properly, a Patent PreCheck analysis report becomes something an attorney, a judge, or a USPTO examiner can point to as evidence of conception date. That is enormous. The more people use it, the more it becomes the standard format for that evidence. Network effects on the legal record side are extremely defensible \u2014 once attorneys start citing Patent PreCheck reports in filings, competitors face a \"why would I use anything else\" dynamic.\n\nThis means: every report should be dated, digitally signed, reference-able by a unique ID, and retrievable. You become the Wayback Machine for IP conception records.\n\n---\n\n**Layer 2 \u2014 Assisted Filing (the natural next step)**\n\nYou're right that this is valuable, but the power isn't just in filing \u2014 it's in the **continuity of evidence chain**. Consider this flow:\n\n- User uploads code \u2192 gets score\n- Does the interactive review \u2192 strengthens their record\n- Gets a PDF summary \u2192 that PDF becomes the invention disclosure document\n- Patent PreCheck pre-fills a provisional patent application from the analysis \u2192 user reviews and submits\n- We track the provisional filing date, the application number, and link it back to the original analysis\n\nThe user never has to start over with an attorney. The attorney gets a pre-populated invention disclosure with documented human conception evidence already built in. That saves them 3-5 hours of billeting time and the attorney loves you for it.\n\n**Copyright filing** is simpler and faster \u2014 the Copyright Office registration is mostly form-filling. Automating that for $35 plus a service fee on top of your analysis is a natural add-on. The key differentiation is that your copyright registration is tied to your IP analysis, creating a complete record.\n\n---\n\n**Layer 3 \u2014 The Inventor's Notebook (legally defensible, ongoing)**\n\nBefore the US switched to first-to-file in 2013, inventor's notebooks were the primary legal record. They still matter for proving conception and reduction-to-practice, especially in derivation proceedings and disputes. Nobody has digitized this properly.\n\nPatent PreCheck can offer a **secure, dated digital inventor's notebook** where users log decisions, ideas, and development milestones \u2014 timestamped, hash-verified, and tied to their projects. Each entry is a potential conception event. This is the longitudinal complement to the code analysis.\n\nThe moat: once users start logging ongoing decisions in Patent PreCheck's notebook, they can't easily move that history to a competitor. It's a multi-year asset that only grows.\n\n---\n\n**Layer 4 \u2014 Attorney Network and Warm Handoffs**\n\nThe biggest moment of friction in the patent process is the transition from \"I think I should file\" to \"I'm working with an attorney.\" Patent PreCheck can own that moment.\n\nBuild a curated network of IP attorneys who have agreed to accept Patent PreCheck reports as a starting point for their engagement \u2014 reducing their intake time and getting clients ready to file faster. The attorney pays Patent PreCheck a referral fee or a co-marketing arrangement. The user gets a warm introduction to a vetted attorney with their full analysis pre-loaded.\n\nThe moat: attorneys who build their intake workflow around Patent PreCheck reports create a pull dynamic. Their clients come to them already having used Patent PreCheck. That attorney won't recommend a competitor.\n\n---\n\n**Layer 5 \u2014 Enterprise and Team Features**\n\nIndividual developers are the wedge, but the real revenue is companies \u2014 startups with 5-50 engineers all using AI tools to build products. The enterprise offering looks like:\n\n- **Portfolio dashboard** \u2014 see all your projects and their scores in one place\n- **Team conception tracking** \u2014 log who contributed what, when, across a team\n- **IP audit for investors** \u2014 before a Series A, run every codebase through Patent PreCheck to get a full IP health report\n- **Attorney collaboration portal** \u2014 share reports directly with your IP counsel\n\nAn investor doing IP diligence on a startup and finding that all their code has been through Patent PreCheck is a massive trust signal. The company that builds the **standard IP diligence workflow for VC-backed startups** owns a category.\n\n---\n\n**Layer 6 \u2014 Prior Art Watch**\n\nOnce someone has filed a provisional or done an analysis, Patent PreCheck can monitor the patent landscape for prior art that could invalidate their application or inform their claims. This is currently only available to enterprises that can afford PatSnap or Derwent subscriptions.\n\nOffering a simplified version \u2014 \"we'll alert you if a patent is filed that overlaps with SmartQueue v2\" \u2014 turns Patent PreCheck into an ongoing defensive service, not just a filing helper. This is another structurally recurring revenue stream.\n\n---\n\n**Layer 7 \u2014 The Data Flywheel (the longest-term moat)**\n\nThis is the one you protect most carefully and never discuss publicly. Every analysis you run teaches your algorithm something about the relationship between code patterns, human decision documentation, and examiner outcomes. Over time:\n\n- You learn which types of comments and documentation actually strengthen applications that get approved\n- You learn which AI contribution patterns correlate with Section 101 rejections\n- You learn which technology domains have the best approval rates under which examiner art units\n\nNo competitor can replicate 3 years of real outcomes data without 3 years of real users. This is the compounding moat that makes the algorithm genuinely better than anything someone can spin up with Claude tomorrow. But it only works if you start collecting structured outcome data now \u2014 tracking not just the analysis but what happened to the application afterward.\n\n---\n\n**What you never say publicly:**\n\n- How the scoring algorithm works\n- Which sources you monitor\n- That the underlying model is an LLM\n- What the risk flag taxonomy looks like\n- Your outcome correlation data\n\n**What you lead with publicly:**\n\n- The score (the output, not the method)\n- The monitoring guarantee (the service commitment)\n- The record (the legal artifact)\n- The review process (the user experience)\n- The outcomes (anonymized: \"applications that completed our review had X% higher approval rates\")\n\nThe last one is the long game. When you can say \"Patent PreCheck users who completed a full review had measurably better outcomes\" \u2014 with real data \u2014 no one can compete with that.",
